<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><span class="fn org country-name"><span style="font-weight:normal;"><span class="Nastaliq" style="font-size: 125%; font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta4', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq';" title="Nastaliq"><span style="font-size:120%;">د افغانستان اسلامي جمهوریت</span></span><br/>
<span class="nowrap"><i>Da Afġānistān Islāmī Jumhoryat</i><span class="nowrap">  </span><span style="font-size:70%;">(<a href="/wiki/Pashto" title="Pashto">Pashto</a>)</span></span></span></span></div>
</li>

</table>
<p><b>Afghanistan</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="noexcerpt"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/ff/En-us-Afghanistan.ogg" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" data-file-height="11" data-file-width="11" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/11px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/17px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/22px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a><sup><span class="IPA" style="color:#00e;font:bold 80% sans-serif;padding:0 .1em"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Afghanistan.ogg" title="File:En-us-Afghanistan.ogg">i</a></span></sup></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/ or /ə/ 'e' in 'roses'">ᵻ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Pashto" title="Pashto">Pashto</a>/<a href="/wiki/Dari_language" title="Dari language">Dari</a>: <span class="Nastaliq" style="font-size: 125%; font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta4', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq';" title="Nastaliq">افغانستان</span>, <i>Afġānistān</i>), officially the <b>Islamic Republic of Afghanistan</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> located within <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Factbook_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-South_Asia_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-South_Asia-11">[10]</a></sup> It has a population of approximately 32 million, making it the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population" title="List of countries by population">42nd</a> most populous country in the world. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> in the south and east; <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> in the west; <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> in the north; and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> in the far northeast. Its territory covers 652,000 km<sup>2</sup> (252,000 sq mi), making it the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">41st largest</a> country in the world.</p>
<p>Human habitation in Afghanistan dates back to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a> Era, and the country's <a href="/wiki/Geostrategy" title="Geostrategy">strategic</a> location along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> connected it to the cultures of the Middle East and other parts of Asia. Through the ages the land has been home to various peoples and witnessed numerous military campaigns; notably by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Mauryas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic conquest of Afghanistan">Muslim Arabs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Khwarezmia_and_Eastern_Iran" title="Mongol invasion of Khwarezmia and Eastern Iran">Mongols</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="First Anglo-Afghan War">British</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_war_in_Afghanistan" title="Soviet war in Afghanistan">Soviet</a>, and in the modern-era by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Western powers</a>. The land also served as the source from which the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hephthalite_Empire" title="Hephthalite Empire">Hephthalites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffarid_Empire" title="Saffarid Empire">Saffarids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghorids" title="Ghorids">Ghorids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khilji_dynasty" title="Khilji dynasty">Khiljis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hotak_Empire" title="Hotak Empire">Hotaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durrani_dynasty" title="Durrani dynasty">Durranis</a>, and others have risen to form major empires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galvin-PreIslamic_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galvin-PreIslamic-12">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The political history of the modern state of Afghanistan began with the Hotak and Durrani dynasties in the 18th century. In the late 19th century, Afghanistan became a <a href="/wiki/Buffer_state" title="Buffer state">buffer state</a> in the "<a href="/wiki/The_Great_Game" title="The Great Game">Great Game</a>" between <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Third Anglo-Afghan War">Third Anglo-Afghan War</a> in 1919, <a href="/wiki/Amanullah_Khan" title="Amanullah Khan">King Amanullah</a> unsuccessfully attempted to modernize the country. It remained peaceful during <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Zahir_Shah" title="Mohammed Zahir Shah">Zahir Shah</a>'s forty years of monarchy. A series of coups in the 1970s was followed by a <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(1978%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (1978–present)">series of civil wars</a> that devastated much of Afghanistan and continues to this day.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Afghanistan" title="Name of Afghanistan">Name of Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>The name <i>Afghānistān</i> (<a href="/wiki/Pashto" title="Pashto">Pashto</a> |افغانستان) is believed to be as old as the <a href="/wiki/Ethnonym" title="Ethnonym">ethnonym</a> <a href="/wiki/Afghan_(ethnonym)" title="Afghan (ethnonym)"><i>Afghan</i></a>, which is documented in the 10th-century <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a> book <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hudud_ul-%27alam" title="Hudud ul-'alam">Hudud ul-'alam</a></i>. The <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">root</a> name "<a href="/wiki/Afghan" title="Afghan">Afghan</a>" was used <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">historically</a> in reference to a member of the ethnic <a href="/wiki/Pashtuns" title="Pashtuns">Pashtuns</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffix</a> "<a href="/wiki/-stan" title="-stan">-stan</a>" means "place of" in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Language" title="Persian Language">Persian</a>. Therefore, Afghanistan translates to <i>land of the Afghans</i> or, more specifically in a historical sense, to <i>land of the Pashtuns</i>. However, the modern <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Afghanistan" title="Constitution of Afghanistan">Constitution of Afghanistan</a> states that "[t]he word Afghan shall apply to every <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Afghanistan" title="Demographics of Afghanistan">citizen of Afghanistan</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Afghanistan" title="History of Afghanistan">History of Afghanistan</a></div>

<td style="padding:0.4em 0"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shuja_Shah_Durrani_of_Afghanistan_in_1839.jpg"><img alt='"Interior of the palace of Shauh Shujah Ool Moolk, Late King of Cabul"' class="thumbborder" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="968" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Shuja_Shah_Durrani_of_Afghanistan_in_1839.jpg/200px-Shuja_Shah_Durrani_of_Afghanistan_in_1839.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Shuja_Shah_Durrani_of_Afghanistan_in_1839.jpg/300px-Shuja_Shah_Durrani_of_Afghanistan_in_1839.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Shuja_Shah_Durrani_of_Afghanistan_in_1839.jpg/400px-Shuja_Shah_Durrani_of_Afghanistan_in_1839.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></td>

<tr>
<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;font-weight:bold;">
<div class="hlist">
<ul>
<li><img alt="Wikipedia book" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Symbol_book_class2.svg/16px-Symbol_book_class2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Symbol_book_class2.svg/23px-Symbol_book_class2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Symbol_book_class2.svg/31px-Symbol_book_class2.svg.png 2x" title="Wikipedia book" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Book:History_of_Afghanistan" title="Book:History of Afghanistan">Book</a></li>
<li><img alt="Category" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="36" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/24px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/32px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:History_of_Afghanistan" title="Category:History of Afghanistan">Category</a></li>
<li><img alt="Portal" data-file-height="28" data-file-width="32" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/16px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/24px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/32px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 2x" title="Portal" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Afghanistan" title="Portal:Afghanistan">Portal</a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Afghanistan" title="Template:History of Afghanistan"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Afghanistan" title="Template talk:History of Afghanistan"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Afghanistan&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Excavation_(archaeology)" title="Excavation (archaeology)">Excavations</a> of prehistoric sites by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Dupree_(professor)" title="Louis Dupree (professor)">Louis Dupree</a> and others suggest that humans were living in what is now Afghanistan at least 50,000 years ago, and that farming communities in the area were among the earliest in the world. An important site of early historical activities, many believe that Afghanistan compares to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> in terms of the historical value of its archaeological sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JFS_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-JFS-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The country sits at a unique nexus point where numerous civilizations have interacted and often fought. It has been home to various peoples through the ages, among them the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Iranian_peoples" title="Ancient Iranian peoples">ancient Iranian peoples</a> who established the dominant role of <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian languages</a> in the region. At multiple points, the land has been incorporated within large regional empires, among them the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Macedonian Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Indian Maurya Empire</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic conquest of Afghanistan">Islamic Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many empires and kingdoms have also risen to power in Afghanistan, such as the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephthalites" title="Hephthalites">Hephthalites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabul_Shahi" title="Kabul Shahi">Kabul Shahis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffarid_Dynasty" title="Saffarid Dynasty">Saffarids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samanids" title="Samanids">Samanids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghurid_Dynasty" title="Ghurid Dynasty">Ghurids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khilji_dynasty" title="Khilji dynasty">Khiljis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kartids" title="Kartids">Kartids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughals</a>, and finally the <a href="/wiki/Hotak_dynasty" title="Hotak dynasty">Hotak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durrani_dynasty" title="Durrani dynasty">Durrani</a> dynasties that marked the political origins of the modern state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Islamic_period">Pre-Islamic period</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_period_of_Afghanistan" title="Pre-Islamic period of Afghanistan">Pre-Islamic period of Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AsokaKandahar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="717" data-file-width="683" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AsokaKandahar.jpg/220px-AsokaKandahar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AsokaKandahar.jpg/330px-AsokaKandahar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AsokaKandahar.jpg/440px-AsokaKandahar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AsokaKandahar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bilingual (<a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a>) <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">edict</a> by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a> from the 3rd century BCE discovered in the southern city of <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">Archaeological</a> exploration done in the 20th century suggests that the geographical area of Afghanistan has been closely connected by culture and trade with its neighbors to the east, west, and north. Artifacts typical of the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_age" title="Iron age">Iron ages</a> have been found in Afghanistan. Urban civilization is believed to have begun as early as 3000 BCE, and the early city of <a href="/wiki/Mundigak" title="Mundigak">Mundigak</a> (near <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a> in the south of the country) may have been a colony of the nearby <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization" title="Indus Valley Civilization">Indus Valley Civilization</a>. More recent findings established that the Indus Valley Civilisation stretched up towards modern-day Afghanistan, making the ancient civilisation today part of Pakistan, Afghanistan and India. In more detail, it extended from what today is northwest Pakistan to northwest India and northeast Afghanistan. An Indus Valley site has been found on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxus_River" title="Oxus River">Oxus River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Shortugai" title="Shortugai">Shortugai</a> in northern Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Ancient_Indus_pp.1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Ancient_Indus_pp.1-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> There are several smaller IVC colonies to be found in Afghanistan as well.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3095" data-file-width="2080" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg/170px-BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg/255px-BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg/340px-BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BamyanBuddha_Smaller_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of the <a href="/wiki/Buddhas_of_Bamiyan" title="Buddhas of Bamiyan">Buddhas of Bamiyan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Afghanistan" title="Buddhism in Afghanistan">Buddhism</a> was widespread before the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic conquest of Afghanistan">Islamic conquest of Afghanistan</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After 2000 BCE, successive waves of semi-nomadic people from Central Asia began moving south into Afghanistan; among them were many <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European-speaking</a> <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranians</a>. These tribes later migrated further into South Asia, Western Asia, and toward Europe via the area north of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>. The region at the time was referred to as <a href="/wiki/Ariana" title="Ariana">Ariana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JFS_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-JFS-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The religion <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> is believed by some to have originated in what is now Afghanistan between 1800 and 800 BCE, as its founder <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a> is thought to have lived and died in <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a>. Ancient <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Eastern Iranian languages</a> may have been spoken in the region around the time of the rise of Zoroastrianism. By the middle of the 6th century BCE, the Achaemenids overthrew the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> and incorporated <a href="/wiki/Arachosia" title="Arachosia">Arachosia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aria_(satrapy)" title="Aria (satrapy)">Aria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> within its eastern boundaries. An <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">inscription</a> on the tombstone of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I_of_Persia" title="Darius I of Persia">Darius I of Persia</a> mentions the <a href="/wiki/Kabulistan" title="Kabulistan">Kabul Valley</a> in a list of the 29 countries that he had conquered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> and his Macedonian forces arrived to Afghanistan in 330 BCE after defeating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_III_of_Persia" title="Darius III of Persia">Darius III of Persia</a> a year earlier in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Battle of Gaugamela</a>. Following <a href="/wiki/Alexander%27s" title="Alexander's">Alexander's</a> brief occupation, the successor state of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> controlled the region until 305 BCE, when they gave much of it to the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a> as part of an alliance treaty. The Mauryans controlled the area south of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> until they were overthrown in about 185 BCE. Their decline began 60 years after <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a>'s rule ended, leading to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic</a> reconquest by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrians" title="Greco-Bactrians">Greco-Bactrians</a>. Much of it soon broke away from them and became part of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a>. They were defeated and expelled by the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythians</a> in the late 2nd century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LoC-pdf_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-LoC-pdf-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the first century BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> subjugated the region, but lost it to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Parthian" title="Indo-Parthian">Indo-Parthian</a> vassals. In the mid-to-late first century CE the vast <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a>, centered in Afghanistan, became great patrons of Buddhist culture, making <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> flourish throughout the region. The Kushans were overthrown by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanids</a> in the 3rd century CE, though the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Sassanids" title="Indo-Sassanids">Indo-Sassanids</a> continued to rule at least parts of the region. They were followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidarite" title="Kidarite">Kidarite</a> who, in turn, were replaced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephthalites" title="Hephthalites">Hephthalites</a>. By the 6th century CE, the successors to the Kushans and Hepthalites established a small dynasty called <a href="/wiki/Kabul_Shahi" title="Kabul Shahi">Kabul Shahi</a>. Much of the northeastern and southern areas of the country remained dominated by Buddhist culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Habibi_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Habibi-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamization_and_Mongol_invasion">Islamization and Mongol invasion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic conquest of Afghanistan">Islamic conquest of Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Central_Asia" title="Mongol invasion of Central Asia">Mongol invasion of Central Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jama_Masjid_of_Herat_15_08.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2210" data-file-width="2400" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Jama_Masjid_of_Herat_15_08.jpg/220px-Jama_Masjid_of_Herat_15_08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Jama_Masjid_of_Herat_15_08.jpg/330px-Jama_Masjid_of_Herat_15_08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Jama_Masjid_of_Herat_15_08.jpg/440px-Jama_Masjid_of_Herat_15_08.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jama_Masjid_of_Herat_15_08.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friday_Mosque_of_Herat" title="Friday Mosque of Herat">Friday Mosque of Herat</a> is one of the oldest mosques in Afghanistan. (March 1962 photo)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> brought Islam to <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zaranj" title="Zaranj">Zaranj</a> in 642 CE and began spreading eastward; some of the native inhabitants they encountered accepted it while others revolted. The land was collectively recognized by the Arabs as al-Hind due to its cultural connection with <a href="/wiki/Greater_India" title="Greater India">Greater India</a>. Before Islam was introduced, people of the region were mostly Buddhists and Zoroastrians, but there were also <a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nana_(Afghan_goddess)" title="Nana (Afghan goddess)">Nana</a> worshipers, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Afghanistan" title="History of the Jews in Afghanistan">Jews</a>, and others. The <a href="/wiki/Zunbils" title="Zunbils">Zunbils</a> and Kabul Shahi were first conquered in 870 CE by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffarid_Dynasty" title="Saffarid Dynasty">Saffarid</a> Muslims of Zaranj. Later, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samanids" title="Samanids">Samanids</a> extended their Islamic influence south of the Hindu Kush. It is reported that Muslims and non-Muslims still lived side by side in Kabul before the <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavids</a> rose to power in the 10th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elliot-2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elliot-2-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mustawfi_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mustawfi-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elliot-3_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elliot-3-28">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the 11th century, <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_of_Ghazni" title="Mahmud of Ghazni">Mahmud of Ghazni</a> defeated the remaining Hindu rulers and effectively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamized" title="Islamized">Islamized</a> the wider region, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Kafiristan" title="Kafiristan">Kafiristan</a>. Afghanistan became one of the main centers in the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> during this <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavid dynasty</a> was overthrown by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghurid_Dynasty" title="Ghurid Dynasty">Ghurids</a>, who expanded and advanced the already powerful Islamic empire.</p>
<p>In 1219 AD, <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a> army overran the region. His troops are said to have annihilated the Khorasanian cities of Herat and Balkh as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bamyan,_Afghanistan" title="Bamyan, Afghanistan">Bamyan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> The destruction caused by the Mongols forced many locals to return to an agrarian rural society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> Mongol rule continued with the <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> in the northwest while the <a href="/wiki/Khilji_dynasty" title="Khilji dynasty">Khilji dynasty</a> administered the Afghan tribal areas south of the Hindu Kush until the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a>, who established the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid Empire</a> in 1370.</p>
<p>In the early 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> arrived from <a href="/wiki/Fergana" title="Fergana">Fergana</a> and captured Kabul from the <a href="/wiki/Arghun_dynasty" title="Arghun dynasty">Arghun dynasty</a>. In 1526, he invaded <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi</a> in India to replace the <a href="/wiki/Lodi_dynasty" title="Lodi dynasty">Lodi dynasty</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>. Between the 16th and 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Bukhara" title="Khanate of Bukhara">Khanate of Bukhara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavids</a>, and Mughals ruled parts of the territory. Before the 19th century, the northwestern area of Afghanistan was referred to by the regional name <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a>. Two of the four capitals of Khorasan (<a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a>) are now located in Afghanistan, while the regions of <a href="/wiki/Kandahar_Province" title="Kandahar Province">Kandahar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zabul_Province" title="Zabul Province">Zabulistan</a>, Ghazni, Kabulistan, and <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Afghanistan" title="Name of Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/Frontier" title="Frontier">frontier</a> between Khorasan and <a href="/wiki/Hindustan" title="Hindustan">Hindustan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-EI-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Firishta_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Firishta-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hotak_dynasty_and_Durrani_Empire">Hotak dynasty and Durrani Empire</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hotak_dynasty" title="Hotak dynasty">Hotak dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durrani_Empire" title="Durrani Empire">Durrani Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ahmad_Shah_Baba.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="312" data-file-width="208" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ahmad_Shah_Baba.JPG/150px-Ahmad_Shah_Baba.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/Ahmad_Shah_Baba.JPG 1.5x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ahmad_Shah_Baba.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Durrani" title="Ahmad Shah Durrani">Ahmad Shah Durrani</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Durrani_Empire" title="Durrani Empire">last Afghan empire</a> and viewed as <i><a href="/wiki/Father_of_the_Nation" title="Father of the Nation">Father of the Nation</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1709, <a href="/wiki/Mirwais_Hotak" title="Mirwais Hotak">Mirwais Hotak</a>, a local <a href="/wiki/Ghilji" title="Ghilji">Ghilzai</a> tribal leader, successfully rebelled against the Safavids. He defeated <a href="/wiki/George_XI_of_Kartli" title="George XI of Kartli">Gurgin Khan</a> and made Afghanistan independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browne_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browne-34">[33]</a></sup> Mirwais died of a natural cause in 1715 and was succeeded by his brother <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Aziz_Hotak" title="Abdul Aziz Hotak">Abdul Aziz</a>, who was soon killed by Mirwais' son <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Hotak" title="Mahmud Hotak">Mahmud</a> for <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a>. Mahmud led the Afghan army in 1722 to the Persian capital of <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a>, captured the city after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gulnabad" title="Battle of Gulnabad">Battle of Gulnabad</a> and proclaimed himself King of Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browne_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Browne-34">[33]</a></sup> The Afghan dynasty was ousted from Persia by <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a> after the 1729 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Damghan_(1729)" title="Battle of Damghan (1729)">Battle of Damghan</a>.</p>
<p>In 1738, Nader Shah and his <a href="/wiki/Afsharid_dynasty" title="Afsharid dynasty">forces</a> captured Kandahar, the last Hotak stronghold, from Shah <a href="/wiki/Hussain_Hotak" title="Hussain Hotak">Hussain Hotak</a>, at which point the incarcerated 16-year-old <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Durrani" title="Ahmad Shah Durrani">Ahmad Shah Durrani</a> was freed and made the commander of an Afghan regiment. Soon after the Persian and Afghan forces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nader_Shah%27s_invasion_of_India" title="Nader Shah's invasion of India">invaded India</a>. By 1747, the Afghans chose Durrani as their <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brit-Durrani_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brit-Durrani-35">[34]</a></sup> Durrani and his Afghan army conquered much of present-day Afghanistan, Pakistan, the <a href="/wiki/Khorasan_Province" title="Khorasan Province">Khorasan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quhistan" title="Quhistan">Kohistan</a> provinces of Iran, and Delhi in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engels_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engels-36">[35]</a></sup> He defeated the Indian <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">Maratha Empire</a>, and one of his biggest victories was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Panipat_(1761)" title="Battle of Panipat (1761)">1761 Battle of Panipat</a>.</p>
<p>In October 1772, Durrani died of a natural cause and was buried at a site now adjacent to the <a href="/wiki/Shrine_of_the_Cloak" title="Shrine of the Cloak">Shrine of the Cloak</a> in Kandahar. He was succeeded by his son, <a href="/wiki/Timur_Shah_Durrani" title="Timur Shah Durrani">Timur Shah</a>, who transferred the capital of Afghanistan from Kandahar to Kabul in 1776. After Timur's death in 1793, the Durrani throne passed down to his son <a href="/wiki/Zaman_Shah_Durrani" title="Zaman Shah Durrani">Zaman Shah</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Shah_Durrani" title="Mahmud Shah Durrani">Mahmud Shah</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuja_Shah_Durrani" title="Shuja Shah Durrani">Shuja Shah</a> and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Afghan Empire was under threat in the early 19th century by the <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Persians</a> in the west and the <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a> in the east. Fateh Khan, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Barakzai_dynasty" title="Barakzai dynasty">Barakzai tribe</a>, had installed 21 of his brothers in positions of power throughout the empire. After his death, they rebelled and divided up the provinces of the empire between themselves. During this turbulent period, Afghanistan had many temporary rulers until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dost_Mohammad_Khan" title="Dost Mohammad Khan">Dost Mohammad Khan</a> declared himself emir in 1826.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanner_2009_126_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tanner_2009_126-38">[37]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab region</a> was lost to <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh" title="Ranjit Singh">Ranjit Singh</a>, who invaded <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">Khyber Pakhtunkhwa</a> and in 1834 captured the city of <a href="/wiki/Peshawar" title="Peshawar">Peshawar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nalwa_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nalwa-39">[38]</a></sup> In 1837, during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jamrud" title="Battle of Jamrud">Battle of Jamrud</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pass" title="Khyber Pass">Khyber Pass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wazir_Akbar_Khan" title="Wazir Akbar Khan">Akbar Khan</a> and the Afghan army failed to capture the Jamrud fort from the <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Khalsa_Army" title="Sikh Khalsa Army">Sikh Khalsa Army</a>, but killed Sikh Commander <a href="/wiki/Hari_Singh_Nalwa" title="Hari Singh Nalwa">Hari Singh Nalwa</a>, thus ending the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afghan-Sikh_Wars" title="Afghan-Sikh Wars">Afghan-Sikh Wars</a>. By this time the British were advancing from the east and the <a href="/wiki/First_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="First Anglo-Afghan War">first major conflict</a> during the "Great Game" was initiated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chahryar_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chahryar-40">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_influence">Western influence</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/European_influence_in_Afghanistan" title="European influence in Afghanistan">European influence in Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reforms_of_Am%C4%81null%C4%81h_Kh%C4%81n_and_civil_war" title="Reforms of Amānullāh Khān and civil war">Reforms of Amānullāh Khān and civil war</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kandahar-1881.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="490" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Kandahar-1881.jpg/220px-Kandahar-1881.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Kandahar-1881.jpg/330px-Kandahar-1881.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Kandahar-1881.jpg/440px-Kandahar-1881.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kandahar-1881.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Army" title="Afghan National Army">allied forces</a> at Kandahar after the 1880 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kandahar" title="Battle of Kandahar">Battle of Kandahar</a>, during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Second Anglo-Afghan War">Second Anglo-Afghan War</a>. The large defensive wall around the city was removed in the early 1930s by the order of <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Nadir_Shah" title="Mohammed Nadir Shah">King Nadir</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1838, the British marched into Afghanistan and arrested <a href="/wiki/Dost_Mohammad_Khan_(Emir_of_Afghanistan)" title="Dost Mohammad Khan (Emir of Afghanistan)">Dost Mohammad</a>, sent him into exile in India and replaced him with the previous ruler, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Shujah_Durrani" title="Shah Shujah Durrani">Shah Shuja</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ingram1980_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-ingram1980-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ingram1984_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-ingram1984-42">[41]</a></sup> Following an uprising, the <a href="/wiki/1842_retreat_from_Kabul" title="1842 retreat from Kabul">1842 retreat from Kabul</a> of British-Indian forces, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kabul_(1842)" title="Battle of Kabul (1842)">Battle of Kabul</a> that led to its recapture, the British placed Dost Mohammad Khan back into power and withdrew their military forces from Afghanistan. In 1878, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Second Anglo-Afghan War">Second Anglo-Afghan War</a> was fought over perceived Russian influence, <a href="/wiki/Abdur_Rahman_Khan" title="Abdur Rahman Khan">Abdur Rahman Khan</a> replaced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohammad_Ayub_Khan_(Emir_of_Afghanistan)" title="Mohammad Ayub Khan (Emir of Afghanistan)">Ayub Khan</a>, and Britain gained controlled Afghanistan's <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy" title="Foreign policy">foreign relations</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Gandamak" title="Treaty of Gandamak">Treaty of Gandamak</a> of 1879. In 1893, <a href="/wiki/Mortimer_Durand" title="Mortimer Durand">Mortimer Durand</a> made Amir Abdur Rahman Khan sign a controversial agreement in which the ethnic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashtun_people" title="Pashtun people">Pashtun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baloch_people" title="Baloch people">Baloch</a> territories were divided by the <a href="/wiki/Durand_Line" title="Durand Line">Durand Line</a>. This was a standard <a href="/wiki/Divide_and_rule" title="Divide and rule">divide and rule</a> policy of the British and would lead to strained relations, especially with the later new state of Pakistan.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="320" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg/170px-Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg/255px-Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1930s-cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Zahir_Shah" title="Mohammed Zahir Shah">Zahir Shah</a>, the last king of Afghanistan, who reigned from 1933 to 1973.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Third Anglo-Afghan War">Third Anglo-Afghan War</a> and the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Afghan_Treaty_of_1919" title="Anglo-Afghan Treaty of 1919">Treaty of Rawalpindi</a> on 19 August 1919, King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Am%C4%81null%C4%81h_Kh%C4%81n" title="Amānullāh Khān">Amanullah Khan</a> declared Afghanistan a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign</a> and fully <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independent state</a>. He moved to end his country's traditional isolation by establishing diplomatic relations with the international community and, following a 1927–28 tour of Europe and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, introduced several reforms intended to modernize his nation. A key force behind these reforms was <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Tarzi" title="Mahmud Tarzi">Mahmud Tarzi</a>, an ardent supporter of the education of women. He fought for Article 68 of Afghanistan's 1923 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Afghanistan" title="Constitution of Afghanistan">constitution</a>, which made elementary education compulsory. The institution of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> was abolished in 1923.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the reforms that were actually put in place, such as the abolition of the traditional <a href="/wiki/Burqa" title="Burqa">burqa</a> for women and the opening of a number of co-educational schools, quickly alienated many tribal and religious leaders. Faced with overwhelming armed opposition, Amanullah Khan was forced to abdicate in January 1929 after Kabul fell to rebel forces led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habibullah_Kalakani" title="Habibullah Kalakani">Habibullah Kalakani</a>. Prince <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Nadir_Shah" title="Mohammed Nadir Shah">Mohammed Nadir Shah</a>, Amanullah's cousin, in turn defeated and killed Kalakani in November 1929, and was declared King Nadir Shah. He abandoned the reforms of Amanullah Khan in favor of a more gradual approach to modernisation but was assassinated in 1933 by <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Khaliq_Hazara_(assassin)" title="Abdul Khaliq Hazara (assassin)">Abdul Khaliq</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hazara_people" title="Hazara people">Hazara</a> school student.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Zahir_Shah" title="Mohammed Zahir Shah">Mohammed Zahir Shah</a>, Nadir Shah's 19-year-old son, succeeded to the throne and reigned from 1933 to 1973. Until 1946, Zahir Shah ruled with the assistance of his uncle, who held the post of <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Afghanistan" title="Prime Minister of Afghanistan">Prime Minister</a> and continued the policies of Nadir Shah. Another of Zahir Shah's uncles, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Mahmud_Khan" title="Shah Mahmud Khan">Shah Mahmud Khan</a>, became Prime Minister in 1946 and began an experiment allowing greater political freedom, but reversed the policy when it went further than he expected. He was replaced in 1953 by <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Daoud_Khan" title="Mohammed Daoud Khan">Mohammed Daoud Khan</a>, the king's cousin and brother-in-law. Daoud Khan sought a closer relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and a more distant one towards Pakistan. Afghanistan remained neutral and was neither a participant in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> nor aligned with either power bloc in the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. However, it was a beneficiary of the latter rivalry as both the Soviet Union and the United States vied for influence by building Afghanistan's main highways, airports, and other vital infrastructure. On per capita basis, Afghanistan received more Soviet <a href="/wiki/Development_aid" title="Development aid">development aid</a> than any other country. In 1973, while King Zahir Shah was on an official overseas visit, Daoud Khan launched a bloodless coup and became the first <a href="/wiki/President_of_Afghanistan" title="President of Afghanistan">President of Afghanistan</a>. In the meantime, <a href="/wiki/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto" title="Zulfikar Ali Bhutto">Zulfikar Ali Bhutto</a> got neighboring Pakistan involved in Afghanistan. Some experts suggest that Bhutto paved the way for the April 1978 <a href="/wiki/Saur_Revolution" title="Saur Revolution">Saur Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowersox_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowersox-44">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marxist_revolution_and_Soviet_war">Marxist revolution and Soviet war</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Saur_Revolution" title="Saur Revolution">Saur Revolution</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afghan-Soviet_War" title="Afghan-Soviet War">Afghan-Soviet War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Afghanistan" title="Democratic Republic of Afghanistan">Democratic Republic of Afghanistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Afghanistan_(1978%E2%80%9392)" title="History of Afghanistan (1978–92)">History of Afghanistan (1978–92)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Day_after_Saur_revolution_in_Kabul_(773).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="761" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Day_after_Saur_revolution_in_Kabul_%28773%29.jpg/220px-Day_after_Saur_revolution_in_Kabul_%28773%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Day_after_Saur_revolution_in_Kabul_%28773%29.jpg/330px-Day_after_Saur_revolution_in_Kabul_%28773%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Day_after_Saur_revolution_in_Kabul_%28773%29.jpg/440px-Day_after_Saur_revolution_in_Kabul_%28773%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Day_after_Saur_revolution_in_Kabul_(773).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Outside the <a href="/wiki/Arg_(Kabul)" title="Arg (Kabul)">Arg</a> <a href="/wiki/Presidential_palace" title="Presidential palace">Presidential Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>, a day after the April 1978 <a href="/wiki/Saur_Revolution" title="Saur Revolution">Marxist revolution</a> in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Afghanistan" title="List of Presidents of Afghanistan">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Daoud_Khan" title="Mohammed Daoud Khan">Daoud Khan</a> was assassinated along with his entire family.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In April 1978, the communist <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_of_Afghanistan" title="People's Democratic Party of Afghanistan">People's Democratic Party of Afghanistan</a> (PDPA) seized power in Afghanistan in the <a href="/wiki/Saur_Revolution" title="Saur Revolution">Saur Revolution</a>. Within months, opponents of the communist government launched an uprising in eastern Afghanistan that quickly expanded into a <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(1978%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (1978–present)">civil war</a> waged by guerrilla <a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">mujahideen</a> against government forces countrywide. The Pakistani government provided these rebels with covert training centers, while the Soviet Union sent thousands of military advisers to support the PDPA government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> Meanwhile, increasing friction between the competing factions of the PDPA — the dominant <a href="/wiki/Khalq" title="Khalq">Khalq</a> and the more moderate <a href="/wiki/Parcham" title="Parcham">Parcham</a> — resulted in the dismissal of Parchami cabinet members and the arrest of Parchami military officers under the pretext of a Parchami coup.</p>
<p>In September 1979, <a href="/wiki/Nur_Muhammad_Taraki" title="Nur Muhammad Taraki">Nur Muhammad Taraki</a> was assassinated in a coup within the PDPA orchestrated by fellow Khalq member <a href="/wiki/Hafizullah_Amin" title="Hafizullah Amin">Hafizullah Amin</a>, who assumed the presidency. Distrusted by the Soviets, Amin was assassinated by Soviet special forces in December 1979. A Soviet-organized government, led by Parcham's <a href="/wiki/Babrak_Karmal" title="Babrak Karmal">Babrak Karmal</a> but inclusive of both factions, filled the vacuum. Soviet troops were deployed to stabilize Afghanistan under Karmal in more substantial numbers, although the Soviet government did not expect to do most of the fighting in Afghanistan. As a result, however, the Soviets were now directly involved in what had been a domestic war in Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> The PDPA prohibited <a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">usury</a>, declared equality of the sexes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-47">[46]</a></sup> and introduced women to political life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-47">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Operation_Cyclone" title="Operation Cyclone">United States</a> had been supporting anti-Soviet Afghan <i><a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">mujahideen</a></i> and foreign "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afghan_Arab" title="Afghan Arab">Afghan Arab</a>" fighters through Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence" title="Inter-Services Intelligence">ISI</a> as early as mid-1979 (see <i><a href="/wiki/CIA_activities_in_Afghanistan" title="CIA activities in Afghanistan">CIA activities in Afghanistan</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> Billions in cash and weapons, which included over two thousand <a href="/wiki/FIM-92_Stinger" title="FIM-92 Stinger">FIM-92 Stinger</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface-to-air_missiles" title="Surface-to-air missiles">surface-to-air missiles</a>, were provided by the United States and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> to Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Brunei_Times_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Brunei_Times-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Nation_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Nation-50">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_war_in_Afghanistan" title="Soviet war in Afghanistan">Soviet war in Afghanistan</a>, Soviet forces and their proxies committed a genocide against the Afghan people and killed up to 2 million Afghans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:22-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soviet-war-video_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soviet-war-video-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landmines_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-landmines-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> and also displaced about 6 million people who subsequently fled Afghanistan, mainly to <a href="/wiki/Afghans_in_Pakistan" title="Afghans in Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghans_in_Iran" title="Afghans in Iran">Iran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">North-West Frontier Province</a> functioned as an organisational and networking base for the anti-Soviet Afghan resistance, with the province's influential <a href="/wiki/Deobandi" title="Deobandi">Deobandi</a> ulama playing a major supporting role in promoting the 'jihad'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> Faced with mounting international pressure and numerous casualties, the Soviets withdrew in 1989 but continued to support Afghan President <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Najibullah" title="Mohammad Najibullah">Mohammad Najibullah</a> until 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia:Afghanistan:History_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia:Afghanistan:History-60">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war">Civil war</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1989%E2%80%9392)" title="Afghan Civil War (1989–92)">Afghan Civil War (1989–92)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%9396)" title="Afghan Civil War (1992–96)">Afghan Civil War (1992–96)</a></div>
<p>From 1989 until 1992, Najibullah's government tried to solve the ongoing civil war with economic and military aid, but without Soviet troops on the ground. Pakistan's spy agency (<a href="/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence" title="Inter-Services Intelligence">ISI</a>), headed by <a href="/wiki/Hamid_Gul" title="Hamid Gul">Hamid Gul</a> at the time, was interested in a trans-national Islamic revolution which would cover Pakistan, Afghanistan and Central Asia. For this purpose Pakistan masterminded an attack on <a href="/wiki/Jalalabad" title="Jalalabad">Jalalabad</a> for the Mujahideen to establish their own government in Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Najibullah tried to build support for his government by portraying his government as <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamic</a>, and in the 1990 constitution the country officially became an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_state_(government)" title="Islamic state (government)">Islamic state</a> and all references of communism were removed. Nevertheless, Najibullah did not win any significant support, and with the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a> in December 1991, he was left without foreign aid. This, coupled with the internal collapse of his government, led to his ousting from power in April 1992. After the fall of Najibullah's government in 1992, the post-communist <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic State of Afghanistan">Islamic State of Afghanistan</a> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Peshawar_Accord" title="Peshawar Accord">Peshawar Accord</a>, a peace and power-sharing agreement under which all the Afghan parties were united in April 1992, except for the Pakistani supported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hezb-e_Islami" title="Hezb-e Islami">Hezb-e Islami</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gulbuddin_Hekmatyar" title="Gulbuddin Hekmatyar">Gulbuddin Hekmatyar</a>. Hekmatyar started a bombardment campaign against the capital city Kabul, which marked the beginning of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_war_in_Afghanistan_(1992%E2%80%9396)" title="Civil war in Afghanistan (1992–96)">new phase in the war</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Saudi Arabia and Iran supported different Afghan militias<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amin_Saikal_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amin_Saikal-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy_Gutman_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_Gutman-65">[64]</a></sup> and instability quickly developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_National_Security_Archive_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_National_Security_Archive-66">[65]</a></sup> The conflict between the two militias soon escalated into a full-scale war.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="657" data-file-width="994" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg/220px-Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg/330px-Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg/440px-Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kabul_during_civial_war_of_fundamentalists_1993-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A section of Kabul during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_war_in_Afghanistan_(1992%E2%80%931996)" title="Civil war in Afghanistan (1992–1996)">civil war</a> in 1993</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to the sudden initiation of the war, working government departments, police units, and a system of justice and accountability for the newly created Islamic State of Afghanistan did not have time to form. Atrocities were committed by individuals of the different armed factions while Kabul descended into lawlessness and chaos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.284.29-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afghanistan_Justice_Project_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Afghanistan_Justice_Project-67">[66]</a></sup> Because of the chaos, some leaders increasingly had only nominal control over their (sub-)commanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29-68">[67]</a></sup> For civilians there was little security from murder, rape, and extortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29-68">[67]</a></sup> An estimated 25,000 people died during the most intense period of bombardment by Hekmatyar's Hezb-i Islami and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junbish-i_Milli" title="Junbish-i Milli">Junbish-i Milli</a> forces of <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rashid_Dostum" title="Abdul Rashid Dostum">Abdul Rashid Dostum</a>, who had created an alliance with Hekmatyar in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afghanistan_Justice_Project_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Afghanistan_Justice_Project-67">[66]</a></sup> Half a million people fled Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29-68">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Southern and eastern Afghanistan were under the control of local commanders such as <a href="/wiki/Gul_Agha_Sherzai" title="Gul Agha Sherzai">Gul Agha Sherzai</a> and others. In 1994, the <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> (a movement originating from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jamiat_Ulema-e-Islam" title="Jamiat Ulema-e-Islam">Jamiat Ulema-e-Islam</a>-run religious schools for Afghan refugees in Pakistan) also developed in Afghanistan as a political-religious force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matinuddin.2C_Kamal_1999_pp.25_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matinuddin.2C_Kamal_1999_pp.25-69">[68]</a></sup> The Taliban first took control of southern Afghanistan in 1994 and forced the surrender of dozens of local Pashtun leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_.285.29-68">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 1994, forces of <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Massoud" title="Ahmad Shah Massoud">Ahmad Shah Massoud</a> held on to Kabul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amnesty.org_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty.org-70">[69]</a></sup> Rabbani's government took steps to reopen courts, restore law and order, and initiate a nationwide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_process" title="Political process">political process</a> with the goal of national <a href="/wiki/Democratic_consolidation" title="Democratic consolidation">consolidation</a> and democratic elections. Massoud invited Taliban leaders to join the process but they refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taliban_Emirate_and_Northern_Alliance">Taliban Emirate and Northern Alliance</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1996%E2%80%932001)" title="Afghan Civil War (1996–2001)">Afghan Civil War (1996–2001)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Alliance" title="Northern Alliance">Northern Alliance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1996afghan_(1).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1305" data-file-width="1698" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/1996afghan_%281%29.png/220px-1996afghan_%281%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/1996afghan_%281%29.png/330px-1996afghan_%281%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/1996afghan_%281%29.png/440px-1996afghan_%281%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1996afghan_(1).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the situation in Afghanistan in late 1996; <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Massoud" title="Ahmad Shah Massoud">Massoud</a> (red), <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rashid_Dostum" title="Abdul Rashid Dostum">Dostum</a> (green) and <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> (yellow) territories.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Taliban's early victories in late 1994 were followed by a series of defeats that resulted in heavy losses. The Taliban attempted to capture Kabul in early 1995 but were repelled by forces under Massoud. In September 1996, as the Taliban, with military support from Pakistan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-George_Washington_University_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-George_Washington_University-72">[71]</a></sup> and financial support from Saudi Arabia, prepared for another major offensive, Massoud ordered a full retreat from Kabul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> The Taliban seized Kabul in the same month and established the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan</a>. They imposed a strict form of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a>, similar to that found in Saudi Arabia. According to <a href="/wiki/Physicians_for_Human_Rights" title="Physicians for Human Rights">Physicians for Human Rights</a> (PHR), "no other regime in the world has methodically and violently forced half of its population into virtual house arrest, prohibiting them on pain of physical punishment from showing their faces, seeking medical care without a male escort, or attending school"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physicians_for_Human_Rights_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Physicians_for_Human_Rights-74">[73]</a></sup> (this statement, though, was made in 1998, long before the advent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISIS" title="ISIS">ISIS</a> which has imposed even tougher and more violent sharia controls).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kabul_(1992%E2%80%9396)" title="Battle of Kabul (1992–96)">After the fall of Kabul</a> to the Taliban, Massoud and Dostum formed the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Alliance" title="Northern Alliance">Northern Alliance</a>. The Taliban defeated Dostum's forces during the <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Mazar-i-Sharif_(1997%E2%80%9398)" title="Battles of Mazar-i-Sharif (1997–98)">Battles of Mazar-i-Sharif (1997–98)</a>. Pakistan's Chief of Army Staff, <a href="/wiki/Pervez_Musharraf" title="Pervez Musharraf">Pervez Musharraf</a>, began sending thousands of Pakistanis to help the Taliban defeat the Northern Alliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_University_Press_Book_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_University_Press_Book-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-George_Washington_University_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-George_Washington_University-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Geographic_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Geographic-75">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_Commons_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-History_Commons-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maley_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maley-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_Rashid.2FThe_Telegraph-78">[77]</a></sup> From 1996 to 2001, the <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a> network of <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayman_al-Zawahiri" title="Ayman al-Zawahiri">Ayman al-Zawahiri</a> was also operating inside Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-79">[78]</a></sup> This and the fact that around one million Afghans were internally displaced made the United States worry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Geographic_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-National_Geographic-75">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> From 1990 to September 2001, around 400,000 Afghans died in the internal mini-wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 9 September 2001, Massoud was assassinated by two Arab <a href="/wiki/Suicide_attack" title="Suicide attack">suicide attackers</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panjshir_province" title="Panjshir province">Panjshir province</a> of Afghanistan. Two days later, the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> were carried out in the United States. The US government suspected Osama bin Laden as the perpetrator of the attacks, and demanded that the Taliban hand him over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> After refusing to comply, the October 2001 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Operation Enduring Freedom</a> was launched. The majority of Afghans supported the American invasion of their country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-84">[83]</a></sup> During the initial invasion, US and UK forces bombed al-Qaeda training camps. The United States began working with the Northern Alliance to remove the Taliban from power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTOct8_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTOct8-85">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history_.282002.E2.80.93present.29">Recent history (2002–present)</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%9314)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–14)">War in Afghanistan (2001–14)</a>, <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2015%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2015–present)">War in Afghanistan (2015–present)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taliban_insurgency" title="Taliban insurgency">Taliban insurgency</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civilian_casualties_in_the_war_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%9314)" title="Civilian casualties in the war in Afghanistan (2001–14)">Civilian casualties in the war in Afghanistan (2001–14)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="827" data-file-width="1024" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg/250px-Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg/375px-Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg/500px-Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_history_from_2008-2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Collage showing foreign armed force and US diplomat visits to Afghanistan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In December 2001, after the Taliban government was overthrown and the new <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Afghanistan" title="Politics of Afghanistan">Afghan government</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Afghanistan" title="List of Presidents of Afghanistan">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Hamid_Karzai" title="Hamid Karzai">Hamid Karzai</a> was formed, the <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">International Security Assistance Force</a> (ISAF) was established by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council" title="UN Security Council">UN Security Council</a> to help assist the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Hamid_Karzai" title="Presidency of Hamid Karzai">Karzai administration</a> and provide basic security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> Taliban forces also began regrouping inside Pakistan, while more coalition troops entered Afghanistan and began rebuilding the war-torn country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly after their fall from power, the Taliban began an <a href="/wiki/Taliban_insurgency" title="Taliban insurgency">insurgency</a> to regain control of Afghanistan. Over the next decade, ISAF and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Army" title="Afghan National Army">Afghan troops</a> led many offensives against the Taliban but failed to fully defeat them. Afghanistan remains one of the poorest countries in the world due to a lack of foreign investment, <a href="/wiki/Corruption_in_Afghanistan" title="Corruption in Afghanistan">government corruption</a>, and the Taliban insurgency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Afghanistan" title="Politics of Afghanistan">Afghan government</a> was able to build some democratic structures, and the country changed its name to the Islamic Republic of Afghanistan. Attempts were made, often with the support of foreign donor countries, to improve the country's economy, healthcare, education, transport, and agriculture. ISAF forces also began to train the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Security_Forces" title="Afghan National Security Forces">Afghan National Security Forces</a>. In the decade following 2002, over five million <a href="/wiki/Afghan_diaspora" title="Afghan diaspora">Afghans</a> were <a href="/wiki/Repatriation" title="Repatriation">repatriated</a>, including some who were forcefully <a href="/wiki/Deportation" title="Deportation">deported</a> from Western countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2009, a Taliban-led shadow government began to form in parts of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> In 2010, President Karzai attempted to hold <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Peace_Jirga_2010" title="Afghan Peace Jirga 2010">peace negotiations</a> with the Taliban leaders, but the rebel group refused to attend until mid-2015 when the Taliban supreme leader finally decided to back the peace talks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the May 2011 <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Osama_bin_Laden" title="Death of Osama bin Laden">death of Osama bin Laden</a> in Pakistan, many prominent Afghan figures were assassinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AfghamEmbassy_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-AfghamEmbassy-96">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Pakistan_skirmishes" title="Afghanistan–Pakistan skirmishes">Afghanistan–Pakistan border skirmishes</a> intensified and many large scale attacks by the Pakistan-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haqqani_Network" title="Haqqani Network">Haqqani Network</a> also took place across Afghanistan. The United States blamed rogue elements within the Pakistani government for the increased attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US-blames-Pakistan_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-US-blames-Pakistan-97">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> The U.S. government spent tens of billions of dollars on development aid over 15 years and over a trillion dollars on military expenses during the same period. Corruption by Western defense and development contractors and associated Afghans reached unprecedented levels in a country where the national GDP was often only a small fraction of the U.S. government's annual budget for the conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_presidential_election,_2014" title="Afghan presidential election, 2014">2014 presidential election</a> President Karzai left power and <a href="/wiki/Ashraf_Ghani" title="Ashraf Ghani">Ashraf Ghani</a> became President in September 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> The US war in Afghanistan (America's longest war) officially ended on 28 December 2014. However, thousands of US-led NATO troops have remained in the country to train and advise Afghan government forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> The 2001–present war has resulted in over 90,000 direct war-related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civilian_casualties_in_the_war_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="Civilian casualties in the war in Afghanistan (2001–present)">deaths</a>, which includes insurgents, Afghan civilians and government forces. Over 100,000 have been injured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Afghanistan" title="Geography of Afghanistan">Geography of Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="1900" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Afghanistan_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/220px-Afghanistan_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Afghanistan_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/330px-Afghanistan_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Afghanistan_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/440px-Afghanistan_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
