<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations_and_military">Foreign relations and military</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Afghanistan" title="Foreign relations of Afghanistan">Foreign relations of Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Armed_Forces" title="Afghan Armed Forces">Afghan Armed Forces</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_soldiers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2100" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Afghan_soldiers.jpg/220px-Afghan_soldiers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Afghan_soldiers.jpg/330px-Afghan_soldiers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Afghan_soldiers.jpg/440px-Afghan_soldiers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_soldiers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Soldiers of the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Army" title="Afghan National Army">Afghan National Army</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ANA_Commando_Battalion" title="ANA Commando Battalion">ANA Commando Battalion</a> standing in the front</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Afghanistan)" title="Ministry of Foreign Affairs (Afghanistan)">Afghan Ministry of Foreign Affairs</a> is in charge of maintaining the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Afghanistan" title="Foreign relations of Afghanistan">foreign relations of Afghanistan</a>. The state has been a member of the United Nations since 1946. It enjoys strong economic relations with a number of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> and allied states, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="Afghanistan–United States relations">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93United_Kingdom_relations" title="Afghanistan–United Kingdom relations">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Germany_relations" title="Afghanistan–Germany relations">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Turkey_relations" title="Afghanistan–Turkey relations">Turkey</a>. In 2012, the United States designated Afghanistan as a <a href="/wiki/Major_non-NATO_ally" title="Major non-NATO ally">major non-NATO ally</a> and created the <a href="/wiki/U.S.%E2%80%93Afghanistan_Strategic_Partnership_Agreement" title="U.S.–Afghanistan Strategic Partnership Agreement">U.S.–Afghanistan Strategic Partnership Agreement</a>. Afghanistan also has friendly diplomatic relations with neighboring <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Pakistan_relations" title="Afghanistan–Pakistan relations">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Iran_relations" title="Afghanistan–Iran relations">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Turkmenistan#Afghanistan" title="Foreign relations of Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Uzbekistan" title="Foreign relations of Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Tajikistan_relations" title="Afghanistan–Tajikistan relations">Tajikistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93China_relations" title="Afghanistan–China relations">China</a>, and with regional states such as <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93India_relations" title="Afghanistan–India relations">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Bangladesh_relations" title="Afghanistan–Bangladesh relations">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Nepal" title="Foreign relations of Nepal">Nepal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Kazakhstan" title="Foreign relations of Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="Afghanistan–Russia relations">Russia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93United_Arab_Emirates_relations" title="Afghanistan–United Arab Emirates relations">UAE</a>, Saudi Arabia, Iraq, Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Japan_relations" title="Afghanistan–Japan relations">Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93South_Korea_relations" title="Afghanistan–South Korea relations">South Korea</a>. It continues to develop diplomatic relations with other countries around the world.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Assistance_Mission_in_Afghanistan" title="United Nations Assistance Mission in Afghanistan">United Nations Assistance Mission in Afghanistan</a> (UNAMA) was established in 2002 under <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1401" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1401">United Nations Security Council Resolution 1401</a> in order to help the country recover from decades of war. Today, a number of NATO member states deploy about 38,000 troops in Afghanistan as part of the <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">International Security Assistance Force</a> (ISAF).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> Its main purpose is to <a href="/wiki/NATO_Training_Mission-Afghanistan" title="NATO Training Mission-Afghanistan">train</a> the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Security_Forces" title="Afghan National Security Forces">Afghan National Security Forces</a> (ANSF). The <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Armed_Forces" title="Afghan Armed Forces">Afghan Armed Forces</a> are under the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defense_(Afghanistan)" title="Ministry of Defense (Afghanistan)">Ministry of Defense</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Army" title="Afghan National Army">Afghan National Army</a> (ANA) and the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Air_Force" title="Afghan Air Force">Afghan Air Force</a> (AAF). The ANA is divided into 7 major <a href="/wiki/Corps" title="Corps">Corps</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/201st_Corps_(Afghanistan)" title="201st Corps (Afghanistan)">201st Selab ("Flood")</a> in Kabul followed by the 203rd in Gardez, <a href="/wiki/205th_Corps" title="205th Corps">205th Atul ("Hero")</a> in Kandahar, 207th in <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a>, 209th in <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-i-Sharif</a>, and the 215th in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lashkar_Gah" title="Lashkar Gah">Lashkar Gah</a>. The ANA also has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ANA_Commando_Brigade" title="ANA Commando Brigade">commando brigade</a>, which was established in 2007. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afghan_Defense_University" title="Afghan Defense University">Afghan Defense University</a> (ADU) houses various educational establishments for the Afghan Armed Forces, including the <a href="/wiki/National_Military_Academy_of_Afghanistan" title="National Military Academy of Afghanistan">National Military Academy of Afghanistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_enforcement">Law enforcement</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Crime_in_Afghanistan" title="Crime in Afghanistan">Crime in Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Afghanistan" title="Law enforcement in Afghanistan">Law enforcement in Afghanistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_in_Afghanistan" title="Alcohol in Afghanistan">Alcohol in Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ANP_trucks_in_Kunar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/ANP_trucks_in_Kunar.jpg/220px-ANP_trucks_in_Kunar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/ANP_trucks_in_Kunar.jpg/330px-ANP_trucks_in_Kunar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/ANP_trucks_in_Kunar.jpg/440px-ANP_trucks_in_Kunar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ANP_trucks_in_Kunar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Police" title="Afghan National Police">Afghan National Police</a> (ANP) in <a href="/wiki/Kunar_Province" title="Kunar Province">Kunar Province</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Directorate_of_Security" title="National Directorate of Security">National Directorate of Security</a> (NDS) is the nation's domestic <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_agency" title="Intelligence agency">intelligence agency</a>, which operates similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Homeland_Security" title="United States Department of Homeland Security">United States Department of Homeland Security</a> (DHS) and has between 15,000 and 30,000 employees. The nation also has about 126,000 national police officers, with plans to recruit more so that the total number can reach 160,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pellerindate_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pellerindate-145">[144]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Police" title="Afghan National Police">Afghan National Police</a> (ANP) is under the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_the_Interior_(Afghanistan)" title="Ministry of the Interior (Afghanistan)">Ministry of the Interior</a> and serves as a single <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_agency" title="Law enforcement agency">law enforcement agency</a> all across the country. The <a href="/wiki/Afghan_National_Civil_Order_Police" title="Afghan National Civil Order Police">Afghan National Civil Order Police</a> is the main branch of the ANP, which is divided into five Brigades, each commanded by a Brigadier General. These brigades are stationed in Kabul, <a href="/wiki/Gardez" title="Gardez">Gardez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-i-Sharif</a>. Every province has an appointed provincial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chief_of_Police" title="Chief of Police">Chief of Police</a> who is responsible for <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Afghanistan" title="Law enforcement in Afghanistan">law enforcement</a> throughout the province.</p>
<p>The police receive most of their training from Western forces under the <a href="/wiki/NATO_Training_Mission-Afghanistan" title="NATO Training Mission-Afghanistan">NATO Training Mission-Afghanistan</a>. According to a 2009 news report, a large proportion of police officers were illiterate and accused of demanding bribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> Jack Kem, deputy to the commander of NATO Training Mission Afghanistan and Combined Security Transition Command Afghanistan, stated that the literacy rate in the ANP would rise to over 50% by January 2012. What began as a voluntary literacy program became mandatory for basic police training in early 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pellerindate_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pellerindate-145">[144]</a></sup> Approximately 17% of them tested positive for illegal drug use. In 2009, President Karzai created two anti-corruption units within the Interior Ministry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup> Former Interior Minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanif_Atmar" title="Hanif Atmar">Hanif Atmar</a> said that security officials from the US (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FBI" title="FBI">FBI</a>), Britain (<a href="/wiki/Scotland_Yard" title="Scotland Yard">Scotland Yard</a>), and the European Union will train prosecutors in the unit.</p>
<p>All parts of Afghanistan are considered dangerous due to militant activities. Hundreds of Afghan police are <a href="/wiki/List_of_Afghan_security_forces_fatality_reports_in_Afghanistan" title="List of Afghan security forces fatality reports in Afghanistan">killed</a> in the line of duty each year. Kidnapping and robberies are also reported. The <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Border_Police" title="Afghan Border Police">Afghan Border Police</a> (ABP) are responsible for protecting the nation's airports and borders, especially the disputed <a href="/wiki/Durand_Line" title="Durand Line">Durand Line</a> border, which is often used by members of criminal organizations and terrorists for their illegal activities. A report in 2011 suggested that up to 3 million people were involved in the illegal drug business in Afghanistan. Attacks on government employees may be ordered by powerful mafia groups who reside inside and outside the country. Drugs from Afghanistan are exported to neighboring countries and then to other countries. The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Counter_Narcotics_(Afghanistan)" title="Ministry of Counter Narcotics (Afghanistan)">Afghan Ministry of Counter Narcotics</a> is tasked to deal with these issues by bringing to justice major drug traffickers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Women may be tried and convicted for breaking social norms, such as running away from a <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced marriage</a> or abusive husband. Victims of rape may be jailed for having had sex outside of marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_7mar2014_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_7mar2014-149">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Afghanistan" title="Economy of Afghanistan">Economy of Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_pomegranate_processing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Afghan_pomegranate_processing.jpg/220px-Afghan_pomegranate_processing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Afghan_pomegranate_processing.jpg/330px-Afghan_pomegranate_processing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Afghan_pomegranate_processing.jpg/440px-Afghan_pomegranate_processing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_pomegranate_processing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Workers processing <a href="/wiki/Pomegranate_production_in_Afghanistan" title="Pomegranate production in Afghanistan">pomegranates</a> (<i>anaar</i>), which Afghanistan is famous for in Asia</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_women_at_a_textile_factory_in_Kabul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1877" data-file-width="3022" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Afghan_women_at_a_textile_factory_in_Kabul.jpg/220px-Afghan_women_at_a_textile_factory_in_Kabul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Afghan_women_at_a_textile_factory_in_Kabul.jpg/330px-Afghan_women_at_a_textile_factory_in_Kabul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Afghan_women_at_a_textile_factory_in_Kabul.jpg/440px-Afghan_women_at_a_textile_factory_in_Kabul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afghan_women_at_a_textile_factory_in_Kabul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Afghan women at a textile factory in Kabul</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Afghanistan is an impoverished <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Least_developed_country" title="Least developed country">least developed country</a>, one of the world's poorest because of decades of war and lack of foreign investment. As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Afghanistan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the nation's GDP stands at about $60.58 billion with an exchange rate of $20.31 billion, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP per capita</a> is $1,900. The country's exports totaled $2.7 billion in 2012. Its unemployment rate was reported in 2008 at about 35%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Factbook_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-10">[9]</a></sup> According to a 2009 report, about 42% of the population lives on less than $1 a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> The nation has less than $1.5 billion in <a href="/wiki/External_debt" title="External debt">external debt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Factbook_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Afghan economy has been growing at about 10% per year in the last decade, which is due to the infusion of over $50 billion in international aid and remittances from Afghan expats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Factbook_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-10">[9]</a></sup> It is also due to improvements made to the <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Afghanistan" title="Transport in Afghanistan">transportation system</a> and agricultural production, which is the backbone of the nation's economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> The country is known for producing some of the finest <a href="/wiki/Pomegranate_production_in_Afghanistan" title="Pomegranate production in Afghanistan">pomegranates</a>, grapes, apricots, melons, and several other fresh and dry fruits, including nuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup> Many sources indicate that as much as 11% or more of Afghanistan's economy is derived from the cultivation and sale of <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a>, and Afghanistan is widely considered the world's largest producer of opium despite Afghan government and international efforts to eradicate the crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the nation's current account deficit is largely financed with donor money, only a small portion is provided directly to the government budget. The rest is provided to non-budgetary expenditure and donor-designated projects through the United Nations system and non-governmental organizations. The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Finance_(Afghanistan)" title="Ministry of Finance (Afghanistan)">Afghan Ministry of Finance</a> is focusing on improved revenue collection and public sector expenditure discipline. For example, government revenues increased 31% to $1.7 billion from March 2010 to March 2011.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan,_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="255" data-file-width="443" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Afghanistan%2C_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png/220px-Afghanistan%2C_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Afghanistan%2C_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png/330px-Afghanistan%2C_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Afghanistan%2C_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png/440px-Afghanistan%2C_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afghanistan,_Trends_in_the_Human_Development_Index_1970-2010.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Afghanistan, Trends in the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>, 1970–2010</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Da_Afghanistan_Bank" title="Da Afghanistan Bank">Da Afghanistan Bank</a> serves as the central bank of the nation and the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_afghani" title="Afghan afghani">"Afghani"</a> (AFN) is the national currency, with an exchange rate of about 47 Afghanis to <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">1 US dollar</a>. Since 2003, over 16 new banks have opened in the country, including <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_International_Bank" title="Afghanistan International Bank">Afghanistan International Bank</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kabul_Bank" title="Kabul Bank">Kabul Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azizi_Bank" title="Azizi Bank">Azizi Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pashtany_Bank" title="Pashtany Bank">Pashtany Bank</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Chartered_Bank" title="Standard Chartered Bank">Standard Chartered Bank</a>, and <a href="/wiki/First_MicroFinance_Bank-Afghanistan" title="First MicroFinance Bank-Afghanistan">First Micro Finance Bank</a>.</p>
<p>One of the main drivers for the current economic recovery is the return of over 5 million <a href="/wiki/Afghan_diaspora" title="Afghan diaspora">expatriates</a>, who brought with them fresh energy, entrepreneurship and wealth-creating skills as well as much needed funds to start up businesses. For the first time since the 1970s, Afghans have involved themselves in construction, one of the largest industries in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> Some of the major national construction projects include <span class="nowrap">$35 billion</span> New Kabul City next to the capital, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghazi_Amanullah_Khan_City" title="Ghazi Amanullah Khan City">Ghazi Amanullah Khan City</a> near Jalalabad, and Aino Mena in Kandahar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> Similar development projects have also begun in <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazar-e-Sharif" title="Mazar-e-Sharif">Mazar-e-Sharif</a>, and other cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, a number of companies and small factories began operating in different parts of the country, which not only provide revenues to the government but also create new jobs. Improvements to the business environment have resulted in more than $1.5 billion in <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">telecom</a> investment and created more than 100,000 jobs since 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Afghan_rug" title="Afghan rug">Afghan rugs</a> are becoming popular again, allowing many carpet dealers around the country to hire more workers.</p>
<p>Afghanistan is a member of <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">WTO</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_Association_for_Regional_Cooperation" title="South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation">SAARC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_Cooperation_Organization" title="Economic Cooperation Organization">ECO</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_OIC" title="Economy of the OIC">OIC</a>. It holds an observer status in <a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Cooperation_Organisation" title="Shanghai Cooperation Organisation">SCO</a>. Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Zalmai_Rassoul" title="Zalmai Rassoul">Zalmai Rassoul</a> told the media in 2011 that his nation's "goal is to achieve an Afghan economy whose growth is based on trade, private enterprise and investment".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> Experts believe that this will revolutionize the economy of the region. <a href="/wiki/Opium_production_in_Afghanistan" title="Opium production in Afghanistan">Opium production in Afghanistan</a> soared to a record in 2007 with about 3 million people reported to be involved in the business,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> but then declined significantly in the years following.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> The government started programs to help reduce poppy cultivation, and by 2010 it was reported that 24 out of the 34 provinces were free from poppy growing. In June 2012, India advocated for private investments in the resource rich country and the creation of a suitable environment therefor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Afghanistan" title="Mining in Afghanistan">Mining in Afghanistan</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michael_E._O%27Hanlon" title="Michael E. O'Hanlon">Michael E. O'Hanlon</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> estimated that if Afghanistan generates about $10 bn per year from its <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Afghanistan" title="Mining in Afghanistan">mineral deposits</a>, its <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_product" title="Gross national product">gross national product</a> would double and provide long-term funding for Afghan security forces and other critical needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> (USGS) estimated in 2006 that northern Afghanistan has an average <span class="nowrap">2.9 billion (bn) <a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)" title="Barrel (unit)">barrels</a></span> (bbl) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>, 15.7 trillion cubic feet (440 bn m<sup>3</sup>) of natural gas, and <span class="nowrap">562 million bbl</span> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_liquids" title="Natural gas liquids">natural gas liquids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-klett2006_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-klett2006-165">[164]</a></sup> In 2011, Afghanistan signed an oil exploration contract with <a href="/wiki/China_National_Petroleum_Corporation" title="China National Petroleum Corporation">China National Petroleum Corporation</a> (CNPC) for the development of three oil fields along the Amu Darya river in the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>The country has significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, copper, gold, coal, <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a>, and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minerals" title="Minerals">minerals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peters2007_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-peters2007-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bgs_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-bgs-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afghanistan.27s_Mineral_Fortune_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Afghanistan.27s_Mineral_Fortune-167">[166]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Khanashin" title="Khanashin">Khanashin</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbonatite" title="Carbonatite">carbonatite</a> in <a href="/wiki/Helmand_Province" title="Helmand Province">Helmand Province</a> contains 1,000,000 metric tons (1,100,000 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">rare earth elements</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tucker2011_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-tucker2011-168">[167]</a></sup> In 2007, a 30-year lease was granted for the <a href="/wiki/Mes_Aynak#Copper_Mine" title="Mes Aynak">Aynak</a> copper mine to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/China_Metallurgical_Group" title="China Metallurgical Group">China Metallurgical Group</a> for $3 billion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup> making it the biggest foreign investment and private business venture in Afghanistan's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> The state-run <a href="/wiki/Steel_Authority_of_India" title="Steel Authority of India">Steel Authority of India</a> won the mining rights to develop the huge <a href="/wiki/Hajigak_Pass" title="Hajigak Pass">Hajigak</a> iron ore deposit in central Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup> Government officials estimate that 30% of the country's untapped mineral deposits are worth between <span class="nowrap">$900 bn</span> and <span class="nowrap">$3 trillion</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcminerals_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbcminerals-114">[113]</a></sup> One official asserted that "this will become the backbone of the Afghan economy" and a Pentagon memo stated that Afghanistan could become the "Saudi Arabia of lithium".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-risen2010_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-risen2010-172">[171]</a></sup> In a 2011 news story, the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Christian_Science_Monitor" title="The Christian Science Monitor">CSM</a></i> reported, "The United States and other Western nations that have borne the brunt of the cost of the Afghan war have been conspicuously absent from the bidding process on Afghanistan's mineral deposits, leaving it mostly to regional powers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Afghanistan" title="Transport in Afghanistan">Transport in Afghanistan</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air">Air</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Afghanistan" title="List of airports in Afghanistan">List of airports in Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ariana_Afghan_A310-300_F-GEMO.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1214" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Ariana_Afghan_A310-300_F-GEMO.jpg/220px-Ariana_Afghan_A310-300_F-GEMO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Ariana_Afghan_A310-300_F-GEMO.jpg/330px-Ariana_Afghan_A310-300_F-GEMO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Ariana_Afghan_A310-300_F-GEMO.jpg/440px-Ariana_Afghan_A310-300_F-GEMO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ariana_Afghan_A310-300_F-GEMO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Ariana_Afghan_Airlines" title="Ariana Afghan Airlines">Ariana Afghan Airlines</a> (AAA) <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A310" title="Airbus A310">Airbus A310</a> in 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Air transport in Afghanistan is provided by the national carrier, <a href="/wiki/Ariana_Afghan_Airlines" title="Ariana Afghan Airlines">Ariana Afghan Airlines</a> (AAA), and by private companies such as <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Jet_International" title="Afghan Jet International">Afghan Jet International</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Horizon_Airlines" title="East Horizon Airlines">East Horizon Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kam_Air" title="Kam Air">Kam Air</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Airways" title="Pamir Airways">Pamir Airways</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Safi_Airways" title="Safi Airways">Safi Airways</a>. Airlines from a number of countries also provide flights in and out of the country. These include <a href="/wiki/Air_India" title="Air India">Air India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emirates_(airline)" title="Emirates (airline)">Emirates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Air" title="Gulf Air">Gulf Air</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran_Aseman_Airlines" title="Iran Aseman Airlines">Iran Aseman Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_International_Airlines" title="Pakistan International Airlines">Pakistan International Airlines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Airlines" title="Turkish Airlines">Turkish Airlines</a>.</p>
<p>The country has four international airports: <a href="/wiki/Hamid_Karzai_International_Airport" title="Hamid Karzai International Airport">Hamid Karzai International Airport</a> (formerly Kabul International Airport), <a href="/wiki/Kandahar_International_Airport" title="Kandahar International Airport">Kandahar International Airport</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herat_International_Airport" title="Herat International Airport">Herat International Airport</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mazar-e_Sharif_International_Airport" title="Mazar-e Sharif International Airport">Mazar-e Sharif International Airport</a>. There are also around a dozen domestic airports with flights to Kabul and other major cities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail">Rail</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Afghanistan" title="Rail transport in Afghanistan">Rail transport in Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Afghanistan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the country has only two rail links, one a 75 km line from <a href="/wiki/Kheyrabad,_Balkh" title="Kheyrabad, Balkh">Kheyrabad</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Uzbekistan_Friendship_Bridge" title="Afghanistan–Uzbekistan Friendship Bridge">Uzbekistan border</a> and the other a 10 km long line from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toraghundi" title="Toraghundi">Toraghundi</a> to the Turkmenistan border. Both lines are used for freight only and there is no passenger service as of yet. There are various proposals for the construction of additional rail lines in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup> In 2013, the presidents of Afghanistan, Turkmenistan, and Uzbekistan attended the groundbreaking ceremony for a 225 km line between Turkmenistan-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andkhvoy" title="Andkhvoy">Andkhvoy</a>-<a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-i-Sharif</a>-<a href="/wiki/Kheyrabad,_Balkh" title="Kheyrabad, Balkh">Kheyrabad</a>. The line will link at <a href="/wiki/Kheyrabad,_Balkh" title="Kheyrabad, Balkh">Kheyrabad</a> with the existing line to the Uzbekistan border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup> Plans exist for a rail line from Kabul to the eastern border town of <a href="/wiki/Torkham" title="Torkham">Torkham</a>, where it will connect with <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Railways" title="Pakistan Railways">Pakistan Railways</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup> There are also plans to finish a rail line between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khaf,_Iran" title="Khaf, Iran">Khaf, Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a>, Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roads">Roads</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Highway_1_(Afghanistan)" title="Highway 1 (Afghanistan)">Highway 1 (Afghanistan)</a></div>
<p>Traveling by bus in Afghanistan remains dangerous due to militant activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> The buses are usually older model <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_buses" title="Mercedes-Benz buses">Mercedes-Benz</a> and owned by private companies. Serious traffic accidents are common on Afghan roads and highways, particularly on the <a href="/wiki/Kabul%E2%80%93Kandahar_Highway" title="Kabul–Kandahar Highway">Kabul–Kandahar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kabul%E2%80%93Jalalabad_Road" title="Kabul–Jalalabad Road">Kabul–Jalalabad Road</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>Newer automobiles have recently become more widely available after the rebuilding of roads and highways. They are imported from the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> through Pakistan and Iran. As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Afghanistan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, vehicles more than 10 years old are banned from being imported into the country. The development of the nation's road network is a major boost for the economy due to trade with neighboring countries. Postal services in Afghanistan are provided by the publicly owned <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Post" title="Afghan Post">Afghan Post</a> and private companies such as <a href="/wiki/FedEx" title="FedEx">FedEx</a>, <a href="/wiki/DHL_Express" title="DHL Express">DHL</a>, and others.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Communications_in_Afghanistan" title="Communications in Afghanistan">Communications in Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>Telecommunication services in the country are provided by <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Wireless" title="Afghan Wireless">Afghan Wireless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Etisalat" title="Etisalat">Etisalat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roshan_(telco)" title="Roshan (telco)">Roshan</a>, <a href="/wiki/MTN_Group" title="MTN Group">MTN Group</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Telecom" title="Afghan Telecom">Afghan Telecom</a>. In 2006, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Communications_(Afghanistan)" title="Ministry of Communications (Afghanistan)">Afghan Ministry of Communications</a> signed a $64.5 million agreement with <a href="/wiki/ZTE" title="ZTE">ZTE</a> for the establishment of a countrywide <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber_cable" title="Optical fiber cable">optical fiber cable</a> network. As of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Afghanistan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Afghanistan had around 17 million <a href="/wiki/GSM" title="GSM">GSM</a> phone subscribers and over 1 million internet users, but only had about 75,000 fixed telephone lines and a little over 190,000 <a href="/wiki/Code_division_multiple_access" title="Code division multiple access">CDMA</a> subscribers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/3G" title="3G">3G</a> services are provided by Etisalat and MTN Group. In 2014, Afghanistan leased a space <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellite</a> from <a href="/wiki/Eutelsat" title="Eutelsat">Eutelsat</a>, called <a href="/wiki/Eutelsat_48D" title="Eutelsat 48D">AFGHANSAT 1</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afghanistan.27s_maiden_satellite_launched_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Afghanistan.27s_maiden_satellite_launched-181">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Afghanistan" title="Health in Afghanistan">Health in Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_ceremony_of_the_Kandahar_Nursing_and_Midwifery_Institute.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1589" data-file-width="2383" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Opening_ceremony_of_the_Kandahar_Nursing_and_Midwifery_Institute.jpg/220px-Opening_ceremony_of_the_Kandahar_Nursing_and_Midwifery_Institute.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Opening_ceremony_of_the_Kandahar_Nursing_and_Midwifery_Institute.jpg/330px-Opening_ceremony_of_the_Kandahar_Nursing_and_Midwifery_Institute.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Opening_ceremony_of_the_Kandahar_Nursing_and_Midwifery_Institute.jpg/440px-Opening_ceremony_of_the_Kandahar_Nursing_and_Midwifery_Institute.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opening_ceremony_of_the_Kandahar_Nursing_and_Midwifery_Institute.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Opening ceremony at a public health institute in <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>, Afghanistan is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">15th least developed country in the world</a>. The average <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_life_expectancy" title="List of countries by life expectancy">life expectancy</a> is estimated to be around 60 years for both sexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-182">[181]</a></sup> The country has one of the highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternal_mortality" title="Maternal mortality">maternal mortality</a> rate in the world as well as the highest <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a> rate in the world (deaths of babies under one year), estimated in 2015 to be 115.08 deaths/1,000 live births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Factbook_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-10">[9]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Public_Health_(Afghanistan)" title="Ministry of Public Health (Afghanistan)">Ministry of Public Health</a> plans to cut the infant mortality rate to 400 for every 100,000 live births before 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters.com_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-183">[182]</a></sup> The country currently has more than 3,000 <a href="/wiki/Midwifery" title="Midwifery">midwives</a>, with an additional 300 to 400 being trained each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csm_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-csm-184">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of hospitals and clinics have been built over the last decade, with the most advanced treatments being available in Kabul. The <a href="/wiki/French_Medical_Institute_for_Children" title="French Medical Institute for Children">French Medical Institute for Children</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indira_Gandhi_Childrens_Hospital" title="Indira Gandhi Childrens Hospital">Indira Gandhi Childrens Hospital</a> in Kabul are the leading <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_hospital" title="Children's hospital">children's hospitals</a> in the country. Some of the other main hospitals in Kabul include the 350-bed <a href="/wiki/Jamhuriat_Hospital" title="Jamhuriat Hospital">Jamhuriat Hospital</a> and the Jinnah Hospital, which is still under construction. There are also a number of well-equipped military-controlled hospitals in different regions of the country.</p>
<p>It was reported in 2006 that nearly 60% of the population lives within a two-hour walk of the nearest health facility, up from 9% in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAID-Health_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-USAID-Health-185">[184]</a></sup> The latest surveys show that 57% of Afghans say they have good or very good access to clinics or hospitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csm_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-csm-184">[183]</a></sup> The nation has one of the highest incidences of people with <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">disabilities</a>, with around a million people affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup> About 80,000 people are missing limbs; most of these were injured by landmines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup> Non-governmental charities such as <a href="/wiki/Save_the_Children" title="Save the Children">Save the Children</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahboba%27s_Promise" title="Mahboba's Promise">Mahboba's Promise</a> assist orphans in association with governmental structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Demographic_and_Health_Surveys" title="Demographic and Health Surveys">Demographic and Health Surveys</a> is working with the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Institute_of_Health_Management_Research" title="Indian Institute of Health Management Research">Indian Institute of Health Management Research</a> and others to conduct a survey in Afghanistan focusing on <a href="/wiki/Maternal_death" title="Maternal death">maternal death</a>, among other things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MEASUREDHS_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-MEASUREDHS-190">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Afghanistan, vitamin D deficiency affects 73.1% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Afghanistan" title="Education in Afghanistan">Education in Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>Education in the country includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%E2%80%9312_(education)" title="K–12 (education)">K–12</a> and higher education, which is supervised by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education_(Afghanistan)" title="Ministry of Education (Afghanistan)">Ministry of Education</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Higher_Education_(Afghanistan)" title="Ministry of Higher Education (Afghanistan)">Ministry of Higher Education</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[191]</a></sup> The nation's education system was destroyed due to the decades of war, but it began reviving after the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Hamid_Karzai" title="Presidency of Hamid Karzai">Karzai administration</a> came to power in late 2001. More than 5,000 schools were built or renovated in the last decade, with more than 100,000 teachers being trained and recruited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAID-Education_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-USAID-Education-193">[192]</a></sup> More than seven million male and female students are enrolled in schools,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAID-Education_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-USAID-Education-193">[192]</a></sup> with about 100,000 being enrolled in different <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Afghanistan" title="List of universities in Afghanistan">universities around the country</a>; at least 35% of these students are female. As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Afghanistan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are 16,000 schools across Afghanistan. Education Minister <a href="/wiki/Ghulam_Farooq_Wardak" title="Ghulam Farooq Wardak">Ghulam Farooq Wardak</a> stated that another 8,000 schools are required to be constructed for the remaining 3 million children who are deprived of education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wardak_seeks_.243b_in_aid_for_school_buildings_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wardak_seeks_.243b_in_aid_for_school_buildings-194">[193]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saleha_Bayat_Building_at_AUAF_in_Kabul-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Saleha_Bayat_Building_at_AUAF_in_Kabul-2.jpg/220px-Saleha_Bayat_Building_at_AUAF_in_Kabul-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Saleha_Bayat_Building_at_AUAF_in_Kabul-2.jpg/330px-Saleha_Bayat_Building_at_AUAF_in_Kabul-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Saleha_Bayat_Building_at_AUAF_in_Kabul-2.jpg/440px-Saleha_Bayat_Building_at_AUAF_in_Kabul-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saleha_Bayat_Building_at_AUAF_in_Kabul-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/American_University_of_Afghanistan" title="American University of Afghanistan">American University of Afghanistan</a> (AUAF) in Kabul</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kabul_University" title="Kabul University">Kabul University</a> reopened in 2002 to both male and female students. In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/American_University_of_Afghanistan" title="American University of Afghanistan">American University of Afghanistan</a> was established in Kabul, with the aim of providing a world-class, English-language, co-educational learning environment in Afghanistan. The capital of Kabul serves as the learning center of Afghanistan, with many of the best <a href="/wiki/Educational_institution" title="Educational institution">educational institutions</a> being based there. Major universities outside of Kabul include <a href="/wiki/Kandahar_University" title="Kandahar University">Kandahar University</a> in the south, <a href="/wiki/Herat_University" title="Herat University">Herat University</a> in the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Balkh_University" title="Balkh University">Balkh University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kunduz_University" title="Kunduz University">Kunduz University</a> in the north, <a href="/wiki/Nangarhar_University" title="Nangarhar University">Nangarhar University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khost_University" title="Khost University">Khost University</a> in the east. The <a href="/wiki/National_Military_Academy_of_Afghanistan" title="National Military Academy of Afghanistan">National Military Academy of Afghanistan</a>, modeled after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a> at West Point, is a four-year military development institution dedicated to graduating officers for the <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Armed_Forces" title="Afghan Armed Forces">Afghan Armed Forces</a>. The $200 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afghan_Defense_University" title="Afghan Defense University">Afghan Defense University</a> is under construction near <a href="/wiki/Qargha_Reservoir" title="Qargha Reservoir">Qargha</a> in Kabul. The United States is building six faculties of education and five provincial teacher training colleges around the country, two large secondary schools in Kabul, and one school in Jalalabad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAID-Education_193-2"><a href="#cite_note-USAID-Education-193">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>The literacy rate of the entire population has been very low but is now rising because more students go to schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogeneratedmil_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogeneratedmil-195">[194]</a></sup> In 2010, the United States began establishing a number of Lincoln learning centers in Afghanistan. They are set up to serve as programming platforms offering English language classes, library facilities, programming venues, <a href="/wiki/Internet_in_Afghanistan" title="Internet in Afghanistan">Internet</a> connectivity, and educational and other counseling services. A goal of the program is to reach at least 4,000 Afghan citizens per month per location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup> The Afghan National Security Forces are provided with mandatory literacy courses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogeneratedmil_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogeneratedmil-195">[194]</a></sup> In addition to this, <a href="/wiki/Baghch-e-Simsim" title="Baghch-e-Simsim">Baghch-e-Simsim</a> (based on the American <a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street" title="Sesame Street">Sesame Street</a>) was launched in late 2011 to help young Afghan children learn.</p>
<p>In 2009 and 2010, a 5,000 OLPC – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_Laptop_Per_Child" title="One Laptop Per Child">One Laptop Per Child</a> schools deployment took place in Kandahar with funding from an anonymous foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup> The OLPC team seeks local support to undertake larger deployment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks hlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%">
<tr>
<td style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Afghanistan" title="Category:Afghanistan">a series</a> on the</td>
</tr>
<tr>

<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg"><img alt="Flag of Afghanistan.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg/16px-Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg/24px-Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg/32px-Flag_of_Afghanistan.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Afghanistan" title="Portal:Afghanistan">Afghanistan  portal</a></li>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Culture_of_Afghanistan" title="Template:Culture of Afghanistan"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Culture_of_Afghanistan" title="Template talk:Culture of Afghanistan"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Culture_of_Afghanistan&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Afghanistan" title="Culture of Afghanistan">Culture of Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>The Afghan culture has been around for over two millennia, tracing back to at least the time of the Achaemenid Empire in 500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LoC_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-LoC-201">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-202">[201]</a></sup> It is mostly a <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribal_society" title="Tribal society">tribal society</a>, with different regions of the country having their own traditions, reflecting the multi-cultural and multi-lingual character of the nation. In the southern and eastern region the people live according to the <a href="/wiki/Pashtun_culture" title="Pashtun culture">Pashtun culture</a> by following <a href="/wiki/Pashtunwali" title="Pashtunwali">Pashtunwali</a>, which is an ancient way of life that is still preserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup> The remainder of the country is culturally <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a>. Some non-Pashtuns who live in proximity with Pashtuns have adopted Pashtunwali<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banting_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banting-204">[203]</a></sup> in a process called <a href="/wiki/Pashtunization" title="Pashtunization">Pashtunization</a> (or <i>Afghanization</i>), while some Pashtuns have been <a href="/wiki/Persianization" title="Persianization">Persianized</a>. Millions of Afghans who have been living in Pakistan and Iran over the last 30 years have been influenced by the cultures of those neighboring nations.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tribal_and_religious_leaders_in_southern_Afghanistan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="1565" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Tribal_and_religious_leaders_in_southern_Afghanistan.jpg/170px-Tribal_and_religious_leaders_in_southern_Afghanistan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Tribal_and_religious_leaders_in_southern_Afghanistan.jpg/255px-Tribal_and_religious_leaders_in_southern_Afghanistan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Tribal_and_religious_leaders_in_southern_Afghanistan.jpg/340px-Tribal_and_religious_leaders_in_southern_Afghanistan.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tribal_and_religious_leaders_in_southern_Afghanistan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Men wearing traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashtun_dress" title="Pashtun dress">Afghan dress</a> in the southern city of <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Afghans display pride in their culture, nation, ancestry, and above all, their religion and independence. Like other highlanders, they are regarded with mingled apprehension and condescension, for their high regard for personal honor, for their tribe loyalty and for their readiness to use force to settle disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heathcote_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heathcote-205">[204]</a></sup> As tribal warfare and internecine feuding has been one of their chief occupations since time immemorial, this individualistic trait has made it difficult for foreigners to conquer them. Tony Heathcote considers the tribal system to be the best way of organizing large groups of people in a country that is geographically difficult, and in a society that, from a materialistic point of view, has an uncomplicated lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heathcote_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heathcote-205">[204]</a></sup> There are an estimated 60 major <a href="/wiki/Pashtun_tribes" title="Pashtun tribes">Pashtun tribes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> and the Afghan nomads are estimated at about 2–3 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>The nation has a complex history that has survived either in its current cultures or in the form of various languages and monuments. However, many of its historic monuments have been damaged in recent wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup> The two famous <a href="/wiki/Buddhas_of_Bamiyan" title="Buddhas of Bamiyan">Buddhas of Bamiyan</a> were destroyed by the Taliban, who regarded them as <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatrous</a>. Despite that, archaeologists are still finding Buddhist relics in different parts of the country, some of them dating back to the 2nd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup> This indicates that Buddhism was widespread in Afghanistan. Other historical places include the cities of <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghazni" title="Ghazni">Ghazni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif" title="Mazar-i-Sharif">Mazar-i-Sharif</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zarang" title="Zarang">Zarang</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Minaret_of_Jam" title="Minaret of Jam">Minaret of Jam</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hari_River,_Afghanistan" title="Hari River, Afghanistan">Hari River</a> valley is a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage site</a>. A cloak reputedly worn by Islam's prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> is kept inside the <a href="/wiki/Shrine_of_the_Cloak" title="Shrine of the Cloak">Shrine of the Cloak</a> in Kandahar, a city founded by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander</a> and the first capital of Afghanistan. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citadel_of_Herat" title="Citadel of Herat">citadel of Alexander</a> in the western city of Herat has been renovated in recent years and is a popular attraction for tourists. In the north of the country is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shrine_of_Hazrat_Ali" title="Shrine of Hazrat Ali">Shrine of Hazrat Ali</a>, believed by many to be the location where <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a> was buried. The Afghan Ministry of Information and Culture is renovating 42 historic sites in Ghazni until 2013, when the province will be declared as the capital of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Afghanistan" title="National Museum of Afghanistan">National Museum of Afghanistan</a> is located in Kabul.</p>
<p>Although literacy is low, classic <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pashto_literature_and_poetry" title="Pashto literature and poetry">Pashto poetry</a> plays an important role in the Afghan culture. <a href="/wiki/Poetry_of_Afghanistan" title="Poetry of Afghanistan">Poetry</a> has always been one of the major educational pillars in the region, to the level that it has integrated itself into culture. Some notable poets include <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rabi%27a_Balkhi" title="Rabi'a Balkhi">Rabi'a Balkhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sanai" title="Sanai">Sanai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jami" title="Jami">Jami</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khushal_Khan_Khattak" title="Khushal Khan Khattak">Khushal Khan Khattak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rahman_Baba" title="Rahman Baba">Rahman Baba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khalilullah_Khalili" title="Khalilullah Khalili">Khalilullah Khalili</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Parween_Pazhwak" title="Parween Pazhwak">Parween Pazhwak</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_entertainment">Media and entertainment</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Afghanistan" title="Media of Afghanistan">Media of Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farhad_Darya%27s_Peace_Concert_in_2010-cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2051" data-file-width="2871" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Farhad_Darya%27s_Peace_Concert_in_2010-cropped.jpg/220px-Farhad_Darya%27s_Peace_Concert_in_2010-cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Farhad_Darya%27s_Peace_Concert_in_2010-cropped.jpg/330px-Farhad_Darya%27s_Peace_Concert_in_2010-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Farhad_Darya%27s_Peace_Concert_in_2010-cropped.jpg/440px-Farhad_Darya%27s_Peace_Concert_in_2010-cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farhad_Darya%27s_Peace_Concert_in_2010-cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Farhad_Darya" title="Farhad Darya">Farhad Darya</a> performing at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serena_Hotel" title="Serena Hotel">Serena Hotel</a> in Kabul.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Afghan mass media began in the early 20th century, with the first newspaper published in 1906. By the 1920s, <a href="/wiki/Radio_Kabul" title="Radio Kabul">Radio Kabul</a> was broadcasting local radio services. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afghanistan_National_Television" title="Afghanistan National Television">Afghanistan National Television</a> was launched in 1974 but was closed in 1996 when the media was tightly controlled by the Taliban.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dart_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-dart-214">[213]</a></sup> Since 2002, press restrictions have been gradually relaxed and private media diversified. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_expression" title="Freedom of expression">Freedom of expression</a> and the press is promoted in the 2004 constitution and censorship is banned, although <a href="/wiki/Defamation" title="Defamation">defaming</a> individuals or producing material contrary to the principles of Islam is prohibited. In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Reporters_Without_Borders" title="Reporters Without Borders">Reporters Without Borders</a> ranked the media environment as 156 out of 173 countries, with the 1st being the most free. Around 400 publications were registered, at least 15 local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_television_channels_in_Afghanistan" title="List of television channels in Afghanistan">Afghan television channels</a>, and 60 radio stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fh_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-fh-215">[214]</a></sup> Foreign radio stations, such as <a href="/wiki/Voice_of_America" title="Voice of America">Voice of America</a>, <a href="/wiki/BBC_World_Service" title="BBC World Service">BBC World Service</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe/Radio_Liberty" title="Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty">Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty</a> (RFE/RL) broadcast into the country.</p>
<p>The city of Kabul has been home to many musicians who were masters of both traditional and modern <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Afghanistan" title="Music of Afghanistan">Afghan music</a>. Traditional music is especially popular during the <a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Nowruz</a> (New Year) and <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Independence_Day" title="Afghan Independence Day">National Independence Day</a> celebrations. <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Zahir" title="Ahmad Zahir">Ahmad Zahir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nashenas" title="Nashenas">Nashenas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Hussain_Sarahang" title="Mohammad Hussain Sarahang">Ustad Sarahang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rahim_Sarban" title="Abdul Rahim Sarban">Sarban</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ubaidullah_Jan" title="Ubaidullah Jan">Ubaidullah Jan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Farhad_Darya" title="Farhad Darya">Farhad Darya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Naghma" title="Naghma">Naghma</a> are some of the notable Afghan musicians, but there are many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup> Most Afghans are accustomed to watching Indian <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> films and listening to its <a href="/wiki/Filmi" title="Filmi">filmi</a> hit songs. Many major Bollywood film stars have roots in Afghanistan, including <a href="/wiki/Salman_Khan" title="Salman Khan">Salman Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saif_Ali_Khan" title="Saif Ali Khan">Saif Ali Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Rukh_Khan" title="Shah Rukh Khan">Shah Rukh Khan</a> (SRK), <a href="/wiki/Aamir_Khan" title="Aamir Khan">Aamir Khan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feroz_Khan_(Indian_actor)" title="Feroz Khan (Indian actor)">Feroz Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kader_Khan" title="Kader Khan">Kader Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naseeruddin_Shah" title="Naseeruddin Shah">Naseeruddin Shah</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zarine_Khan" title="Zarine Khan">Zarine Khan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celina_Jaitly" title="Celina Jaitly">Celina Jaitly</a>. In addition, several Bollywood films, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Dharmatma" title="Dharmatma">Dharmatma</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Khuda_Gawah" title="Khuda Gawah">Khuda Gawah</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Escape_from_Taliban" title="Escape from Taliban">Escape from Taliban</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Kabul_Express" title="Kabul Express">Kabul Express</a></i> have been shot inside Afghanistan.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Afghanistan" title="Sport in Afghanistan">Sport in Afghanistan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ahmad_Faisal_-_football_-_A.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="459" data-file-width="610" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Ahmad_Faisal_-_football_-_A.jpg/220px-Ahmad_Faisal_-_football_-_A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Ahmad_Faisal_-_football_-_A.jpg/330px-Ahmad_Faisal_-_football_-_A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Ahmad_Faisal_-_football_-_A.jpg/440px-Ahmad_Faisal_-_football_-_A.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ahmad_Faisal_-_football_-_A.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_national_football_team" title="Afghanistan national football team">Afghanistan national football team</a> (in red uniforms) before its first win over <a href="/wiki/India_national_football_team" title="India national football team">India</a> (in blue) during the <a href="/wiki/2011_SAFF_Championship" title="2011 SAFF Championship">2011 SAFF Championship</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In recent years, Afghan sports teams have increasingly celebrated titles at international events. Afghanistan's <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_national_basketball_team" title="Afghanistan national basketball team">basketball team</a> won the first team sports title at the <a href="/wiki/2010_South_Asian_Games" title="2010 South Asian Games">2010 South Asian Games</a>. Later that year, the country's <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_national_cricket_team" title="Afghanistan national cricket team">cricket team</a> followed as it won the 2010 <a href="/wiki/ICC_Intercontinental_Cup" title="ICC Intercontinental Cup">ICC Intercontinental Cup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[216]</a></sup> In 2012, the country's <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_national_3x3_team" title="Afghanistan national 3x3 team">3x3 basketball team</a> won the gold medal at the <a href="/wiki/3-on-3_basketball_at_the_2012_Asian_Beach_Games" title="3-on-3 basketball at the 2012 Asian Beach Games">2012 Asian Beach Games</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[217]</a></sup> in 2013, Afghanistan's <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_national_football_team" title="Afghanistan national football team">football team</a> followed as it won the <a href="/wiki/SAFF_Championship" title="SAFF Championship">SAFF Championship</a>.</p>
<p>Cricket is the country's most popular sport, followed by <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">association football</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_national_cricket_team" title="Afghanistan national cricket team">Afghan national cricket team</a>, which was formed in the last decade, participated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2009_ICC_World_Cup_Qualifier" title="2009 ICC World Cup Qualifier">2009 ICC World Cup Qualifier</a>, <a href="/wiki/2010_ICC_World_Cricket_League_Division_One" title="2010 ICC World Cricket League Division One">2010 ICC World Cricket League Division One</a> and the <a href="/wiki/2010_ICC_World_Twenty20" title="2010 ICC World Twenty20">2010 ICC World Twenty20</a>. It won the <a href="/wiki/ACC_Twenty20_Cup" title="ACC Twenty20 Cup">ACC Twenty20 Cup</a> in 2007, 2009, 2011 and 2013. The team eventually made it to play in the <a href="/wiki/2015_Cricket_World_Cup" title="2015 Cricket World Cup">2015 Cricket World Cup</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_Cricket_Board" title="Afghanistan Cricket Board">Afghanistan Cricket Board</a> (ACB) is the official governing body of the sport and is headquartered in Kabul. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghazi_Amanullah_Khan_International_Cricket_Stadium" title="Ghazi Amanullah Khan International Cricket Stadium">Ghazi Amanullah Khan International Cricket Stadium</a> serves as the nation's main cricket stadium, followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kabul_National_Cricket_Stadium" title="Kabul National Cricket Stadium">Kabul National Cricket Stadium</a>. Several other stadiums are under construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[219]</a></sup> Domestically, cricket is played between teams from different provinces.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_national_football_team" title="Afghanistan national football team">Afghanistan national football team</a> has been competing in international <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> since 1941. The national team plays its home games at the <a href="/wiki/Ghazi_Stadium" title="Ghazi Stadium">Ghazi Stadium</a> in Kabul, while <a href="/wiki/Football_in_Afghanistan" title="Football in Afghanistan">football in Afghanistan</a> is governed by the <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_Football_Federation" title="Afghanistan Football Federation">Afghanistan Football Federation</a>. The national team has never competed or qualified for the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup</a>, but has recently won an international football trophy in 2013. The country also has a national team in the sport of futsal, a 5-a-side variation of football.</p>
<p>Other popular sports in Afghanistan include basketball, volleyball, <a href="/wiki/Taekwondo" title="Taekwondo">taekwondo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bodybuilding" title="Bodybuilding">bodybuilding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buzkashi" title="Buzkashi">Buzkashi</a> is a traditional sport, mainly among the northern Afghans. It is similar to <a href="/wiki/Polo" title="Polo">polo</a>, played by horsemen in two teams, each trying to grab and hold a goat carcass. The <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Hound" title="Afghan Hound">Afghan Hound</a> (a type of running dog) originated in Afghanistan and was originally used in hunting.</p>

<li id="cite_note-Bowersox-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Bowersox_44-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Bowersox, Gary W. (2004). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WVAN9pjnRzMC&amp;lpg=PP1&amp;pg=PA100#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>The Gem Hunter: The Adventures of an American in Afghanistan</i></a>. United States: GeoVision, Inc.,. p. 100. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-9747323-1-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-9747323-1-1">0-9747323-1-1</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">22 August</span> 2010</span>. <q>To launch this plan, Bhutto recruited and trained a group of Afghans in the <a href="/wiki/Bala_Hissar,_Peshawar" title="Bala Hissar, Peshawar">Bala-Hesar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Peshawar" title="Peshawar">Peshawar</a>, in Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">North-west Frontier Province</a>. Among these young men were <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Massoud" title="Ahmad Shah Massoud">Massoud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulbuddin_Hekmatyar" title="Gulbuddin Hekmatyar">Gulbuddin Hekmatyar</a>, and other members of Jawanan-e Musulman. Massoud's mission to Bhutto was to create unrest in northern Afghanistan. It served Massoud's interests, which were apparently opposition to the Soviets and independence for Afghanistan. Later, after Massoud and Hekmatyar had a terrible falling-out over Massoud's opposition to terrorist tactics and methods, Massoud overthrew from Jawanan-e Musulman. He joined <a href="/wiki/Burhanuddin_Rabbani" title="Burhanuddin Rabbani">Rabani</a>'s newly created Afghan political party, <a href="/wiki/Jamiat-e_Islami" title="Jamiat-e Islami">Jamiat-i-Islami</a>, in exile in Pakistan.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAfghanistan&amp;rft.aufirst=Gary+W.&amp;rft.aulast=Bowersox&amp;rft.btitle=The+Gem+Hunter%3A+The+Adventures+of+an+American+in+Afghanistan&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DWVAN9pjnRzMC%26lpg%3DPP1%26pg%3DPA100%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=0-9747323-1-1&amp;rft.pages=100&amp;rft.place=United+States&amp;rft.pub=GeoVision%2C+Inc.%2C&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-:1-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-:1_51-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Kakar, Mohammed. <a class="external text" href="http://publishing.cdlib.org/ucpressebooks/view?docId=ft7b69p12h;brand=ucpress" rel="nofollow"><i>The Soviet Invasion and the Afghan Response, 1979–1982</i></a>.
